This pass uses a continuously updated key that depends on both the byte position and the execution path taken through the switch statement to decrypt each byte.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 11: Decompiled code of a split transformation decrypting subroutine\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/garble-fig11.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/garble-fig11.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eGoStringUngarbler: Automatic String Deobfuscator\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo systematically approach string decryption automation, we first consider how this can be done manually. From our experience, the most efficient manual approach leverages dynamic analysis through a debugger. Upon finding a decrypting subroutine, we can manipulate the program counter to target the subroutine's entry point, execute until the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eret\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e instruction, and extract the decrypted string from the return buffer.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo perform this process automatically, the primary challenge lies in identifying all decrypting subroutines introduced by garble's transformations. Our analysis revealed a consistent pattern—decrypted strings are always processed through Go's \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eruntime_slicebytetostring\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e function before being returned by the decrypting subroutine. This observation provides a reliable anchor point, allowing us to construct regular expression (regex) patterns to automatically detect these subroutines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eString Encryption Subroutine Patterns\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThrough analyzing the disassembled code, we have identified consistent instruction patterns for each string transformation variant. For each transformation on 64-bit binaries, \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003erbx\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is used to store the decrypted string pointer, and \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ercx\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is assigned with the length of the decrypted string. The main difference between the transformations is the way these two registers are populated before the call to \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eruntime_slicebytetostring\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e# Go compiler v1.21 -\u0026gt; v1.23 (x64)\r\n\r\n# Stack Transformation Epilogue Pattern \r\n\r\n48 8D 5C ?? ??     lea     rbx, [rsp+\u0026lt;num\u0026gt;]            # decrypted string pointer\r\nB9 ?? ?? ?? ??     mov     ecx, \u0026lt;num\u0026gt;                  # decrypted string length\r\nE8 ?? ?? ?? ??     call    runtime_slicebytetostring\r\n48 83 ?? ??        add     rsp, \u0026lt;num\u0026gt;                  # epilogue clean up\r\n5D                 pop     rbp\r\nC3                 retn\r\n\r\n---------------------------------------\r\n# Split Transformation Epilogue Pattern\r\n\r\n31 C0              xor     eax, eax\r\n48 89 ??           mov     rbx, \u0026lt;reg\u0026gt;                  # decrypted string pointer\r\n48 89 ??           mov     rcx, \u0026lt;reg\u0026gt;                  # decrypted string length\r\nE8 ?? ?? ?? ??     call    runtime_slicebytetostring\r\n48 83 ?? ??        add     rsp, \u0026lt;num\u0026gt;\r\n5D                 pop     rbp\r\nC3                 retn\r\n\r\n---------------------------------------\r\n# Seed Transformation Epilogue Pattern\r\n\r\n48 8B ??           mov     rbx, [\u0026lt;reg\u0026gt;]                # decrypted string pointer\r\n48 8B ?? ??        mov     rcx, [\u0026lt;reg\u0026gt;+8]              # decrypted string length\r\n31 C0              xor     eax, eax\r\nE8 ?? ?? ?? ??     call    runtime_slicebytetostring\r\n48 83 ?? ??        add     rsp, \u0026lt;num\u0026gt;\r\n5D                 pop     rbp\r\nC3                 retn\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp style\u003d\"text-align:center\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eFigure 12: \u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eEpilogue patterns of garble’s\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003edecrypting subroutines\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThrough the assembly patterns in Figure 12, we develop regex patterns corresponding to each of garble's transformation types, which allows us to automatically identify string decrypting subroutines with high precision.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo extract the decrypted string, we must find the subroutine’s prologue and perform instruction-level emulation from this entry point until \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eruntime_slicebytestring\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is called. For binaries of Go versions v1.21 to v1.23, we observe two main patterns of instructions in the subroutine prologue that perform the Go stack check.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e# Go prologue pattern 1\r\n\r\n49 3B ?? ??             cmp     rsp, [\u0026lt;reg\u0026gt;+\u0026lt;num\u0026gt;]\r\n0F 86 ?? ?? ?? ??       jbe     \u0026lt;offset\u0026gt;\r\n\r\n------------------------\r\n# Go prologue pattern 2\r\n\r\n49 3B ?? ??             cmp     rsp, [\u0026lt;reg\u0026gt;+\u0026lt;num\u0026gt;]\r\n76 ??                   jbe     short \u0026lt;offset\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp style\u003d\"text-align:center\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eFigure 13: \u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ePrologue instruction patterns of Go subroutines\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThese instruction patterns in the Go prologue serve as reliable entry point markers for emulation. The implementation in GoStringUngarbler leverages these structural patterns to establish reliable execution contexts for the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.unicorn-engine.org/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"font-style:italic;text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eunicorn\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e emulation engine\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e, ensuring accurate string recovery across various garble string transformations.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFigure 14 shows the output of our automated extraction framework, where GoStringUngarbler\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eis able to identify and emulate all decrypting subroutines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 14: GoStringUngarbler’s string extraction output\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/original_images/garble-fig14.gif",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/original_images/garble-fig14.gif"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFrom these instruction patterns, we have derived a YARA rule for detecting samples that are obfuscated with garble’s literal transformation. The rule can be found in \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/mandiant/gostringungarbler\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant's GitHub repository\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eDeobfuscation: Subroutine Patching\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWhile extracting obfuscated strings can aid malware detection through signature-based analysis, this alone is not useful for reverse engineers conducting static analysis. To aid reverse engineering efforts, we've implemented a binary deobfuscation approach leveraging the emulation results. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAlthough developing an IDA plugin would have streamlined our development process, we recognize that not all malware analysts have access to, or prefer to use, IDA Pro. To make our tool more accessible, we developed GoStringUngarbler as a standalone Python utility to process binaries protected by garble. The tool can deobfuscate and produce functionally identical executables with recovered strings stored in plain text, improving both reverse engineering analysis and malware detection workflows.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFor each identified decrypting subroutine, we implement a strategic patching methodology, replacing the original code with an optimized stub while padding the remaining subroutine space with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eINT3\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e instructions (Figure 15).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003exor     eax, eax                           ; clear return register\r\nlea     rbx, \u0026lt;string addr\u0026gt;                 ; Load effective address of decrypted string \r\nmov     ecx, \u0026lt;string len\u0026gt;                  ; populate string length\r\ncall    runtime_slicebytetostring          ; convert slice to Go string\r\nret                                        ; return the decrypted string\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp style\u003d\"text-align:center\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eFigure 15: \u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFunction stub to patch over garble’s decrypting subroutines\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eInitially, we considered storing recovered strings within an existing binary section for efficient referencing from the patched subroutines. However, after examining obfuscated binaries, we found that there is not enough space within existing sections to consistently accommodate the deobfuscated strings. On the other hand, adding a new section, while feasible, would introduce unnecessary complexity to our tool.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eInstead, we opt for a more elegant space utilization strategy by leveraging the inherent characteristics of garble's string transformations. In our tool, we implement in-place string storage by writing the decrypted string directly after the patched stub, capitalizing on the guaranteed available space from decrypting routines:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eStack transformation:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e The decrypting subroutine stores and processes encrypted strings on the stack, providing adequate space through their data manipulation instructions. The instructions originally used for pushing encrypted data onto the stack create a natural storage space for the decrypted string.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSeed transformation:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e For each character, the decrypting subroutine requires a call instruction to decrypt it and update the seed. This is more than enough space to store the decrypted bytes.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSplit transformation:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e The decrypting subroutine contains multiple switch cases to handle fragmented data recovery and decryption. These extensive instruction sequences guarantee sufficient space for the decrypted string data.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFigure 16 and Figure 17 show the disassembled and decompiled output of our patching framework, where GoStringUngarbler\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ehas deobfuscated a decrypting subroutine to display the recovered original string.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 16: Disassembly view of a deobfuscated decrypting garble\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003epresents a specific challenge—its dependence on the Go compiler for obfuscation means that the calling convention can evolve between Go versions. This change can potentially invalidate the regular expression patterns used in our deobfuscation process. To mitigate this, we've designed GoStringUngarbler\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ewith a modular plugin architecture. This allows for new plugins to be easily added with updated regular expressions to handle variations introduced by new Go releases. This design ensures the tool's long-term adaptability to future changes in garble’s output.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCurrently, GoStringUngarbler\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eprimarily supports garble\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e-\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eobfuscated PE and ELF binaries compiled with Go versions 1.21 through 1.23.
